1400052181|5.0|Henrietta Lacks was a poor African-American woman who died in 1951 at the age of 31 from cervical cancer. She was treated at Johns Hopkins Hospital in Baltimore, where it was discovered that she had an amazingly aggressive tumor and was in agonizing pain. While she lay dying, some of her cells, both healthy and diseased, were extracted from her for further use in research. This was done without her knowledge or permission, or the knowledge of her family. This was actually common procedure at the time. At the time, permission was not required.<br /><br />Others cells in other patients had not worked. Most cells will divide for a certain number of times and then die. But for some reason, Henrietta's cells were amazingly vital and survived. Her cells were dubbed \\"HeLa\\". HeLa cells were the first human cell line to prove successful in vitro, in test tubes or Petri dishes , which has had profound impact on medical research. For example, Jonas Salk used HeLa cells to test the first polio vaccine. They have also been used in cancer and AIDS research. HeLa cells were dubbed \\"immortal\\" because they could be divided an infinite number of times. They still exist, in various forms, today.<br /><br />Henrietta's family knew nothing about the HeLa cells until many years later. Here is where the story gets even more complicated: the original doctor, George Gey, seems to have had no bad motives. He was completely dedicated to medicine, and simply wanted the cells for further research. Gey freely donated the cells to other researchers. However, it was other companies who obtained HeLa cells who made money off of products invented because of the cells. Henrietta was forgotten or unknown to most of the scientists and researchers. While some corporations made millions, Henrietta's family, while proud that her cells have helped countless others, have remained poor.<br /><br />The whole idea of medical ethics is enormously complicated. Was racism involved? Was it because Henrietta was poor and black? Or was it simply in the interest of science and medicine? What about the companies who benefitted financially from the research? Are cells still harvested today without permission? The answers are not as simple as they may seem.<br /><br />This book studies medical ethics, family history, racism, and poverty. Author Rebecca Skloot does a fine job in researching and setting forth her arguments. This book was obviously a real labor of love and she is passionately devoted to the subject. Skloot became so involved with the family that she set up a foundation to help them: The Henrietta Lacks Foundation.<br /><br />If I have any quibbles about this book, it is that it is a bit too long. I did make my husband read the book. It is not at all the sort of book he usually reads, but he is a scientist and he found it fascinating.<br /><br />I also highly recommend this book. It is not light reading, but it is undeniably intriguing and important.<br /><br />Henrietta's cells have helped many scientists and researchers develop new ways of dealing with disease. In this way, Henrietta Lacks has become truly immortal.

0345803507|3.0|As most of you probably know, Fifty Shades Freed is the final installment in the Fifty Shades trilogy, and for my money, it is entertaining if you have enjoyed the series-to-date, but also entirely too expected.<br />What started out as an interesting, dark and psychologically twisted sexual tale, soon becomes a story about change, love and all of that mushy unrealistic crap that's penned in some of these fictional romances.<br />To my mind, Christian Grey is annihilated in character within these pages, turned by affection and what he believes is the weakness of his past. Fifty Shades Freed was the lightest of all novels with the predictable happy ending. Again, this is only going to be a three out of five fangs rating for me but this is based on my earlier interest in the much more complex, self-serving and charismatic sadist known as Christian Grey.

0316228532|2.0|THE CASUAL VACANCY, Rowling's first novel for an adult audience, takes place in an English village called Pagford.  A member of the parish council, Barry Fairbrother, has died suddenly of a brain aneurism, leaving the casual vacancy - the British term for the need to fill a vacated seat on the parish council.<br /><br />Pagford seems like the perfect little English village, but it is far from it.  With politics being politics anywhere, readers see the people of the parish council quarreling among themselves, particularly over what people in the U.S. would call a housing project between Pagford and Yarvil, and the effectiveness of a drug rehabilitation center which the Pagford government controls.<br /><br />There's more:  Parents and teens disagree; this is natural, too, but for some families in the Pagford area there are serious consequences. Spouses have horrible quarrels among themselves, also with serious consequences.  There is indeed domestic abuse in this novel, as well as drug, alcohol, and tobacco use/abuse, teen and adult sexuality, and much foul language.<br /><br />The characters impress me as rather flat and stock.  There is the overweight parish councilor who feels that his son is an effeminate slacker.  There is the woman doctor with three children, one of whom is not as clever as the other two and is constantly put down for it, not just by her mother but by her schoolmates.  There is the bad girl from the wrong side of town with the big heart who is caring for her nursery-age brother because of her mother's drug habit.<br /><br />But the real mystery (at least, as far as the members of the Parish Council):  Who is posting the \\"Ghost of Barry Fairbrother\\" posts on the council website?  Is it one person, or several?  Why is this person/ these people doing this?<br /><br />So, in all honesty, Rowling has given us a Peyton Place in a British village. This book just didn't work for me, and I think - know! - that Rowling can do a lot better than this.

0374214913|3.0|Mr. Penumbra is one of those books that start a story with a quite good concept. The central narrative is crafted well. The scenes are described concretely and with attractive texture. I think the characters, however, came and went without much, if any, depth.<br /><br />But I am willing to be charitable on character. This is a fantasy tale, in the best sense of the word -- and fantasies can be skimpy on characterization. Its narrative leads to some ultimate statements that may or may not correspond to reality: this is expected, of course, in the fantasy genre.<br /><br />Here is the intriguing point. That very possibility -- that the final revelation of the novel's central gnosis is less than metaphysical -- is itself the most fantastical notion of the book.<br /><br />Nevertheless, this most fantastical conclusion is articulated in a way that does not do justice to the richness of everything the writer has invested in the tale -- even his less-than-solid characterizations.<br /><br />I enjoyed Mr. Penumbra. However, it joins an ever-lengthening shelf of recent books that promise a lot at the outset, and even succeed at establishing an attractive world, but do not advance a conclusion that is worthy of the beginning.

B005ER6STG|5.0|The first thing you will immediately notice about A Serbian Film is the quality of the production and acting. For a film of this nature, one would generally expect a B-movie atmosphere. This is simply not the case, and is perhaps the one major reason this film has intrigued me so much. The character development in the early portion of the film was also surprisingly well done, and was very convincing to boot. It has been a long while since I've seen a good horror film that allowed me to feel something for the characters on screen. The family is relatively normal, albeit Milos' taboo job of being a porn star.<br /><br />As far as porn is concerned, we see very little gratuity in this area. Certainly there is nudity, but it is mostly done in a tasteful fashion. It is a paradox of some sort. To see this piece of cinema that undoubtedly falls under the moniker of \\"torture porn,\\" yet so much is held back. Indeed, the gore and the themes presented are taboo and will not appeal to everyone. The film is genuinely intense. However, I did not feel that the gore or the nudity itself was the reason why this film was intense. While there are a few scenes that will stick in mind for a long time, the overall somber atmosphere and psychological torture you (as the viewer) will undergo is what I found most gripping.<br /><br />When it comes to adult themes, this film holds nothing back. We see rape, child rape, genital torture, and similar things. Still, so little is shown. But, what will stick with you is what is said. The final words in the film change the dynamic of the movie, make it far more disturbing, and ultimately cause this film to be one of my favorites in the horror/thriller genre.<br /><br />So, in conclusion, if you can stomach a good amount of gore, don't mind gratuitous nudity, and are interested in seeing a movie explore such adult themes, you will enjoy this for sure.

B008O9V4C2|4.0|It all starts with a signature groove so nasty it could be a Bond villain and before you can shout \\"Goldfinger\\" we are deep into the new Donald Fagen LP \\"Sunken Condo's\\". With the \\"Nightfly Trilogy\\" now safely tucked away the erstwhile Steely Dan frontman is free to cast away the concept shackles and provide a straightforward set of expertly delivered jazzy rock tinged with soul. Whether he and Walter Becker will ever follow up the decade old \\"Everything must go\\" is a moot point but frankly as long as both artists produce wonderful solo material from time to time we should pay homage to the mighty forces. There have been some reviews claiming that \\"SC\\" ranks alongside \\"The Nightfly\\" as Fagen's best solo effort to date. This suggestion deserves outright scepticism since his first solo album recorded nearly 30 years ago remains peerless and his latest doesn't match it. What \\"Sunken Condo's\\" does represent is further evidence of that unswerving and alarming quality control that Fagen has achieved on all his albums ensuring that nearly all of them remain head and shoulders above most artists. It's all about cool detachment, a sprinkling of cynicism and songs like onions that you peel away and discover different layers. By doing so they reveal something slightly quirky and original. The delight of an unnoticed backbeat, deep bass run or a jazzy guitar phrase so that every listen sucks you in to Fagen's sheer level of musical craft.<br /><br />The albums opener is the louche jazz of \\"Slinky thing\\". It has everything you would expect from Fagen present and correct. The band is tighter than a gnat's chuff, lyrically we have females standing next to a \\"Reptile cage\\" and great understated guitar solos from Jon Herington. Next up \\"Not the same without you\\" is so pure Steely Dan it could have been on \\"Gaucho\\" where ironically forty years after \\"Can't buy a thrill\\" Fagen tells us \\"that I'm evolving now/at a really astounding rate of speed\\". Who are we to argue? Third track \\"Memorabilia\\" is one of those upon first listens seems rather repetitive but stick with it since it turns out to be a lovely song. \\"Weather in my head\\" is one of the toughest urban blues songs Fagen has penned and references Al Gore with great horns from co-producer Michael Leonheart, while \\"New Breed\\" has one of those slight reggae vibes which are a specialty of our hero and echoes \\"Gaslighting Abbie\\". One bump in the road is the cover of Issac Hayes funk anthem \\"Out of the Ghetto\\" which would no doubt make a great encore in a live show but doesn't really fit here. Do not fret too much however since Fagen follows this with his best song in many a long year and the albums highlight. \\"Miss Marlene\\". This is a brilliant and effortless collection of jazzy grooves so infectious they could cause a pandemic and his acerbic nasal croon is as good as ever. A fine Fagen lyric underpins it and the band is cut loose resulting in some of the greatest \\"Dan style\\" instrumentation since \\"Aja\\". Download it immediately and think kindly of your reviewer. More cool nasty funk follows on the truly excellent \\"Good stuff\\" a song which deals with the shipping of an illegal special brew which requires that \\"Tonight we jack the convoy/ Two hundred barrel run /Trucked in from Agooey/In East Patterson\\". Closer \\"Planet D'rhonda\\" has a superb solo at its heart from jazz guitar giant Kurt Rosenwinke (thank you Erik) but at this stage it sounds like one of the weaker songs on the album.<br /><br />So there it is. The master returns with yet another dose of what Adam Sweeting has described with pinpoint precision as \\"a set of impeccably tailored grooves which wear their expertise with insolent nonchalance\\". \\"Sunken Condo's\\" also confirms that there are a generation of musicians like Donald Fagen who qualify for retirement but who refuse to lie down and indeed seem to be thriving into the third age. This album is quiet treat and you really would be very foolish not to let it soundtrack your daily existence for a short while.nce for a short while.

0062073486|5.0|I cannot say that I am Agatha Christie's number one fan in the world simply because I am not the number one fan of the mystery genre.  But I must tell you that when I do read a mystery I like the sort of story that features deductive reasoning in solving the mystery rather than the plethora of such novels involving scientific police procedures.  I think the term that suits me best as far as such things go is \\"reactionary,\\" at worse and \\"old fashion\\" at best.  I am a big Arthur Conan Doyle fan for that very reason so even though \\"mysteries\\" per se are not at the top of my list, I do enjoy reading them from time to time.  Over the years I have read a number of Christie's book and have yet to be disappointed in any of them.  This, my third reading of \\"And Then There Were None.\\" is no exception<br /><br />I will say that this work is a bit different than Christie's other work in that there is no detective to \\"solve the case.\\"  The reader is put on the spot to supply their own deductive reasoning to work out \\"who done it.\\"  I rather like this approach for a change.<br /><br />Ten people are invited to an isolated house located on Indian Island.  No one else is in the house or on the island.  One by one the guests, after each has been rather uniquely accused of murder.  And then one by one the guests begin dying...murdered.  One of the guests is the murderer!<br /><br />The author has done a very nice job, in my opinion, of putting this plot together.  While she possibly could have done better is the area of character development, this work, when you consider its length, is really not set up for that.  The overriding theme is murder and who did it.  I personally was unable to figure out who the killer was, but then I am not the brightest star in the sky and am most certainly not an expert on murder mysteries.  Perhaps others will do better.<br /><br />This is an interesting book for a couple of other reasons.  Since it was first published in England in 80 some odd years ago it has gone through at least three title changes and has, via various editions been bowdlerized - to the extreme.  This all has been done out of political correctness and belated racial sensitivities.  While Christie was most certainly a brilliant writer, she was also most certainly a product of her times when it came to racial issues and it shows. The original English edition never made it to this country, as far as I know, and if the original title was kept there is no way on earth that it would be published in this day and age.<br /><br />This is one of Christie's better known novels and there have been a number of plays and movies made from it.  Some folks love this novel; others are not so fond of it.  It is one of my personal favorites of her work but then I have not read every one of her books so I cannot really say it is the best or worse. B003UESJH4|5.0|Colin Firth is Prince Albert, who suffers from a speech stammer that has kept him and his family largely away from the public spotlight. But when his reckless brother steps down from the throne, he finds himself in a position he never thought he'd be; leader of an empire. But with trouble brewing overseas and his new position demanding that he makes speeches to the public, he turns to Lionel Logue (Geoffrey Rush), an Australian speech therapist, for help and the two form an unlikely friendship.<br /><br />If you haven't already figured out from the 5-stars and the heading, I absolutely loved `The King's Speech', it's beautifully shot, the production is gorgeous and is anchored by stellar performances from some of UK's, and Australia's, top actors.<br /><br />Colin Firth is at his best here as the suffering Prince Albert, conveying layers of emotion both in voice and body language and getting us emotionally connected with Albert and we're really rooting for him to overcome his stammer by the end. Geoffrey Rush is equally as good as Lionel Logue; confident, straight forward and unwilling to kowtow to Albert's status and when the two share a scene together, it's electrifying to watch as their friendship develops throughout the film.<br /><br />Helena Bonham Carter, away from the weird-wacky Tim Burton roles also shines as Albert's supportive wife, Elizabeth, as does Guy Pearce, playing a brash Edward VIII.<br /><br />It's not just the strong acting, although it's really what drives the film; it's the production design, the sets, the costumes really do a great job at bringing this world back to life. They also made sure to flesh out the world by surrounding it in history, with the threat of World War II always looming in the background, and to represent the attitudes of the people at the time, especially the way the English looked down on Australians. Everything here pulls together to make this a compelling and elegant film, if you like period movies or shows like `Downton Abbey', then this is worth the watch, if not the purchase, and is deserving of the four academy awards under it's belt.<br /><br />There's a sleuth of Special Features on the disc; a commentary track, an okay behind the scenes featurette and `BBC America Special' with Q&A with the director and cast. The more interesting features are `The Personal Diaries', an interview clip with Lionel Logue's grandson about his grandfather and how he came to access materials that helped him write a book about Lionel and King George VI Archive, featuring real speech clips of Prince Albert.

B003UYUP6M|5.0|This is a fascinating book, and I say that as a Professor of Comparative Literature, not as a techie.  It is extremely interesting to see all of the mathematical and conceptual problems that had to be solved in order to create the search engine that Google is today.  It gave me a whole new respect for the end product.  It is equally fascinating to see the founders and their idiosyncracies, and their insistence on bringing ethics to commerce.  One example is how they sell ads:  The top spot does not necessarily go to the highest bidder but rather to the most relevant ad;  it is possible for an ad paying 10 cents a word to \\"win\\" over an ad paying 50 cents if the former's content is more appropriate to the topic in question.  The user is always right as a corporate model is intriguing.  The book is also amusing, with Sergey Brin dashing in and out on his roller blades, and various other quirks that make Google fun for the reader as well as for the people working there.  Finally, it is rewarding to see two smart guys who had set out to get their PhDs, not to make money, succeed; it is also exciting to see a dedication to excellence, real quality control in hiring the best people available, and in their demands on their own product.  A fast-paced, interesting, and appealing book, even for someone who was not initially drawn to the subject matter.

In all energy exchanges, if no energy enters or leaves an isolated system, the entropy of that system increases. New energy cannot be created and high-grade energy is being destroyed.\\"<br /><br />Like all other Muse albums, this one takes a different turn in terms of sound. As a matter of fact, it takes several different turns, as I will describe below with a track-by-track breakdown:<br /><br />1) \\"Supremacy\\": What's Led Zeppelin's \\"Kashmir\\" + a dark James Bond theme song + Matthew Bellamy's falsetto? Muse's \\"Supremacy,\\" which starts their new album off with a bang of massive proportions. This is the perfect opening song for \\"The 2nd Law,\\" as it provides a grand reflection of the band throughout their history, yet with an expansion of that said history and with a hint of possibly what's to come.<br /><br />Grade: 10.00/10.00<br /><br />2) \\"Madness\\": The first single off the album provides for quite the change-up, as it directly follows the hard-rocking \\"Supremacy\\" with a slow, '80s pop/dance feel to it initially, before Bellamy breaks into a solid guitar solo and the building instrumentation builds up into a great climax, where Bellamy yet again shows off his one-of-a-kind vocals.<br /><br />Grade: 7.75/10.00<br /><br />3) \\"Panic Station\\": If there's going to be a #1 smash-hit off this album, this will be that song. The bassline would make Flea of the Red Hot Chili Peppers realize he no longer needed Viagra. It feels and sounds like an '80s dance song with a Muse tinge to it. When first hearing this song, I had to check the album information again to make certain it wasn't released in 1983. This is definitely a change of pace for the band, but as is typical with Muse, the experimentation paid off and I have a hunch this is going to be a radio hit in the States.<br /><br />Grade: 9.75/10.00<br /><br />4/5) \\"Prelude + Survivor\\": This was to be the theme song for the Olympics in London this past summer. For such an epic event, the song matched it on that front and then-some. Bellamy's piano prelude perfectly introduces the song and as is common with Muse songs, the song gradually builds until we reach an epic climax. The lyrics are a bit on the cheesy side, but lyrics aside, the song and all its components, sounds great. From the piano prelude to Bellamy's spot-on vocals to his guitar solos and beyond, it's yet another great epic rock song conducted by the band.<br /><br />Grade: 8.00/10.00<br /><br />6) \\"Follow Me\\": Like with \\"Madness\\" and \\"Panic Station,\\" this has an '80s feel to it as well. Believe it or not, but one can hear a Gloria Gaynor influence (\\"I Will Survive\\") just prior to the onset of the chorus. While there was worry amongst fans that the band was going full dub-step with this album, that certainly wasn't the case. It is included in this song, however, and to great effect. Like with \\"Panic Station,\\" it may be difficult for people to not start dancing when hearing this song, especially if they've been introduced to the three wise men that night (Johnny Walker, Jim Beam and Jack Daniels).<br /><br />Grade: 7.50/10.00<br /><br />7) \\"Animals\\": This has a very classic rock, almost bluesy feel to it. The song gradually builds into a catchy, grand climax, where we can hear clamoring Wall Street stockbrokers. The lyrics are some of the best and most topical on the album, I feel.<br /><br />Toward the tail-end of the song, Bellamy belts out:<br /><br />\\"Out of control,<br />We're out of control,<br />Crush those who beg at your feet,<br />Analyze,<br />Franchise,<br />Spread out,<br />Kill the competition, and buy yourself an ocean<br /><br />Amortize,<br />Downsize,<br />Lay off,<br />Kill yourself,<br />Come on and do us all a favor.\\"<br /><br />Grade: 9.50/10.00<br /><br />8) \\"Explorers\\": This is one of the slowest songs off the album, but one which continues to grow with me. Supposedly, lead singer Matthew Bellamy wrote this for his child - a lullaby of sorts. This may very well be the song most influenced by Queen on this album, yet as Muse seems to always do regardless of the influence, they make the sound all theirs.<br /><br />Grade: 7.75/10.00<br /><br />9) \\"Big Freeze\\": U2 appears to be the main influence for this song, especially with regard to the guitars. The song starts off as an '80s tune one may want to forget, yet a catchy chorus and strong second half of the song make up for it. I feel this is one of the band's weaker efforts on the album, but is still a decent song truth be told.<br /><br />Grade: 7.25/10.00<br /><br />10) \\"Save Me\\": This is the first of two songs written and sung by bassist Chris Wolstenholme - the first two he's written and sung for the band. Both songs deal with his personal struggles pertaining to alcoholism.<br /><br />In the song, he sings:<br /><br />\\"Hold me cause I'm sure I hated<br />Promises they are overrated<br />Wait just a while<br />While I'm drowning in denial\\"<br /><br />It's a very slow, almost '60 or perhaps '70s rock song, that eventually picks up toward the song's mid-portion. While I don't think it's a bad song by any stretch and I can completely understand Wolstenholme wanting to express his personal battle and accomplishment with overcoming alcoholism, I do feel this is probably the weakest song on the album. He does have a decent voice - James Maynard-esque perhaps - and there is a genuineness to his words, yet even taking those two components into consideration, I feel this is the weakest song on \\"The 2nd Law.\\"<br /><br />Grade: 6.00/10.00<br /><br />11) \\"Liquid State\\": This is the second of the two Wolstenholme-led songs and provides a stark contrast to the first. It's arguably the heaviest song on the album and carries with it an '80s heavy metal feel - Metallica perhaps. Like with \\"Save Me,\\" it delves into the bassist's battle with alcoholism, as he sings:<br /><br />\\"Warm my heart to remind<br />And hold my head up high<br />And help me to survive<br /><br />Kick me when I'm down<br />Feed me poison, for me till I drown<br />Wake me up before I get punched out and fall into the night\\"<br /><br />In the end, I feel this is a much stronger effort from Wolstenholme and should provide for a good live tune as well.<br /><br />Grade: 8.50/10.00<br /><br />12) \\"The 2nd Law: Unsustainable\\": It was due to this song rumors circulated the internet that Muse had gone dub-step. The dub-step doesn't kick in until a little before the midway point. The song starts in a tense, dramatic fashion, as the listener can feel, like in a movie, something bad is about to occur. The instrumental then hits a certain point of tension, where it can't go any further and breaks into dub-step. This is quite symbolic I feel, as the dub-step is representative of total and utter chaos - all the while a robotic voice utters the word unsustainable. While I love the opening to the song and appreciate the symbolism behind the dub-step, I still can't seem to completely warm up to that and due to that, feel it's probably one of the weaker tracks on the album.<br /><br />Grade: 7.00/10.00<br /><br />13) \\"The 2nd Law: Isolated System\\": Here's the second of the two instrumentals and in my opinion, provides a perfect close to the album. The sound is slower than the preceding instrumental, and slightly haunting. It provides the sound of a dark, dramatic film and the feel that chaos has struck and we're suddenly at a loss on where to turn next. In the second-half of the song, however, things begin to pick up and a sound of hope and optimism can be heard, signifying that while chaos has struck, we can still pull through it.<br /><br />Grade: 8.25/10.00<br /><br />Overall: While some may not like the fact that so many different musical genres have been presented by Muse on this disc and that it may disrupt the flow on the album, I think it's refreshing and more times than not, the band is able to pull off these experimentations in sound with seeming ease. One can't help but admire a band willing to continually alter their sound, expand their musical horizons in an attempt to take them above and beyond where they had ever been previously as musicians. From one album to the next, that is what Muse does - not showcasing any fear of losing long-time fans or of critical backlash. Every album, every song is a new journey with the band and while I and others may on occasion not like a particular sound experimented by the band, I have far more respect for musicians like Muse who continually push themselves than those like Nickelback who have seemed to master the art of legally ripping off themselves and for some strange reason, found success in doing so.<br /><br />Grade Scale: 50.0 - 59.9% = Very average - Siskel and Ebert would have given it one thumb up and even then, the thumb wouldn't be raised very high.<br /><br />60.0 - 69.9% = Above average - Siskel and Ebert would have given this one very enthusiastic thumb up. I don't believe they ever rated a movie in such a manner, but I'll do that for them now. You're welcome.<br /><br />70.0% - 74.9% = Good - It may not be a masterpiece, but is better than most. On the KA (Kick A**) scale, it'd cover one cheek and perhaps lightly graze the other.<br /><br />75.0 - 79.9% = Very good - About 4/5 of both cheeks would be kicked with this kind of score.<br /><br />80.0 - 84.9% = Great - Both cheeks would be fully kicked and while Siskel and Ebert may not have given it two very enthusiastic thumbs, the two thumbs would raise and with perhaps at least a skosh of enthusiasm.<br /><br />85.0 - 89.9% = Excellent - On the edge of being a masterpiece, but one key component is missing from it being called as such. This score would be deserving of both cheeks being kicked via the KA scale, with the kicker throwing in a \\"Hi-yah!\\" for good measure.<br /><br />90.0 - 100.0% = Masterpiece - This place is reserved for the best of the best. It's where the likes of Nickelback, Limp Bizkit, N'Sync, Kriss Kross, Vanilla Ice and \\"American Idol\\" winners will never see their names.<br /><br />My \\"The 2nd Law\\" Grade: 97.25/120.00 = 81.04% (Great)

0553801473|3.0|It is understandable, when one considers the fact of how long a gap there was between \\"Feast for crows\\" and \\"A Dance with Dragons\\", that many people are going to be disappointed. When you're an author, you can't have it both ways - you have to choose/make compromise between what hard-core fans expect and your own artistic integrity and/or vision. Before the HBO show started, I wasn't sure that anyone still reading this series isn't a hard-core fan - you'd have to be pretty persistent to remain around Westeros for this long. Nowadays, things are a bit different. I don't consider myself to be a hard-core fan of this series. I liked what Martin did in first three books, I even liked the fourth one (despite angry mob being angry for no apparent reason) but the fifth one takes the whole \\"oh my god moment\\" to a new level. After waiting for so many years one can't but be disappointed. There are many good things in this book, Martin still knows his storytelling. Overall, though, it's a giant jumble in a dire need of a proficient editor.<br /><br /> Anyway, there are many things one can talk about concerning this book and it's hard to choose between them. Space on the Amazon is rather short and one should avoid technicalities and ramblings. I've yet to learn how to avoid latter. Let's try to think about facts we have at hand. \\"A dance with dragons\\" is 5th book in a series of seven. That means 71% of the Saga is completed. By the way, I won't be putting spoiler tags so tread carefully. So then, what is yet to happen? Eventually, Danny has to return to Westeros and conquer some towns. Preferably the one with Iron Throne in it. Of course, there are Others and impounding battle between forces of Ice and Fire. This is bound to have a lasting impact on an entire universe. Arya has to grow up eventually, return to Westeros as well, and do some avenging. Spider and Littlefinger have to hatch whatever plot they're hatching and so on. There are a number of open story-threads that need to be closed.<br /><br /> Average number of pages per book (up to this point) is around 850. \\"A dance with dragons\\" alone has 1056. That is almost 25% more than average. So we have the longest book yet in which several things happen. Many severals in fact. Most of them are people eating and parading around. There are bits of this and that scattered around as well. What is important to note is this. In this book, Danny had to assert control on Meereen (a single town), Stannis had to rally support of North (by conquering a single town), Jon had to build up his defenses, Victarion had to sail across the sea, and the other Targaryen had to be pulled out of the ass to further disintegrate the plot. Thousand pages later, nothing of this actually happened. Danny is no longer in Meereen (dragons magically grew up despite the fact that in some earlier book someone mentioned that this would require years) but battle there is far from over. Stannis got stuck in a snow storm beneath the walls of Winterfell. Arya had two chapters in which she did some killing but essentially run around in circles. Varys appeared, oh how conveniently, out of the blue to kill Kevan Lannister (because he can). Victarion just barely made it to the other shore, and Jon was stuck on the wall and ended up repeatedly stabbed. Oh, and Bran met some nice tree and had a little chat with him. Basically, in 1056 pages key characters moved few miles accomplishing meager things. It sort of reminds me of some conservative chess openings that I've seen people do just to irritate their opponents.<br /><br />Remember what is yet to happen? Remember that there are only two books left? What can we conclude from known facts? There are several options. If pace remains the same, upcoming books will be 4k+ pages mammoths with people running around avoiding to do things. If average page count remains the same we can expect the pace becoming lightning quick with people teleporting around doing things they should've beendoing all this time. Or, we can expect that seventh book won't be the final one. In any of these cases, when one has in mind the speed of Martin's writing and/or publishing of these books, we can assume that we will never see the ending of these series. If we actually do, it will be far from satisfying.<br /><br /> Martin needs an Editor. Martin needs and Editor which will beat him with a stick whenever he sees him writing another paragraph on honeyed locusts, roasted dogs, kidney puddings and Coat of Arms of some minor lordling with a complete history of his minor House. What we need isn't another \\"Wheel of Time\\". What we got with this book is something just like that - A giant filler full of random ramblings (Pretty Pig and the whole Tyrion story arc - seriously?) that make you wonder why are you still reading this book, and why will you read the next one when it comes out in a couple of years. B000VLE3GA|5.0|Fan's are gonna argue over this album for the next forty years, fighting over what should and shouldn't be on a greatest hits [of sorts] release.  And I use the term `greatest hits' lightly, because Led Zeppelin were never really a band who put out singles, preferring to let each album represent itself and the group at that particular moment in time.  If you ask a die-hard Zepper which compilation to score, most people will tell you that if you want a greatest hits release from Led Zeppelin, then you should save up and purchase all of their albums.  But that was before the idea of Mothership even existed, Mothership threw all the cards in the air, is a collection of twenty-four of Zep's most loved and timeless numbers, remastered and selected by the band's final three remaining members [Jimmy Page, Robert Plant, and John Paul Jones], and it must be taken seriously for that reason alone ... these songs where chosen by the band themselves, not a record company, not the industry, not simply Jimmy Page, but collectively by the chaps who laid down every single note.<br /><br />There's no bonus material to be found here, if anything, Mothership was designed as a primer for those who perhaps never heard of Led Zeppelin before, and want to get a sense of what all the hub-bub and fuss was all about.  There's not a single thing here for the long time fan, or for anyone who's ever listened to FM radio ... less of course you're a record completist, and then of course you'll need this to fill out that empty quarter inch on your record shelf.  After all, Jimmy Page has put together several compilations over the years, the first being what's come to be known as Crop Circles, a four disc collection, and then there was Early Days & Later Days Volumes I and II, released by Atlantic Records.  Never the less, if nothing else, Mothership will remind everyone that Led Zeppelin will not doubt remain one of the finest bands of all time.<br /><br />Though if you were asking me where to spend my hard earned cash ... I'd point your right here, and never look back!  Definitive Collection Mini LP Replica CD Box Set<br /><br />Review by Jenell Keslerd my hard earned cash ... I'd point your right here, and never look back!  Definitive Collection Mini LP Replica CD Box Set<br /><br />Review by Jenell Kesler

Little Victories isn't about the usual country fluff, Little Victories is both a step back and a step forward, delivering urgent social commentary through songs with stance and character.<br /><br />Review by Jenell Kesler

1607066017|5.0|For many years, Brian K. Vaughan has been dazzling us and making our heads spin with his wordsmithing and storytelling on some of the greatest comics of the last decade or so. Books like Y: THE LAST MAN, EX MACHINA and his initial run on RUNAWAYS are the stuff that comic book readers' dreams are made of. Alas, since the devastating finale of EX MACHINA, he took some well-deserved time off, but when he announced a new creator-owned series for Image Comics that was apparently going to be \\"STAR WARS meets ROMEO AND JULIET meets GAME OF THRONES\\", people's heads started to explode with anticipation. But would it live up to the inevitable hype surrounding a work by Vaughan and artist Fiona Staples?<br /><br />Short answer: Abso-freakin'-loutely.<br /><br />SAGA is the type of space opera that only someone like Vaughan could do, and do with enough smarts, fun, emotion, action and surprises to keep the audience wrapped around his finger. It's the story of Marko and Alana, a pair of star-cross'd lovers from different sides of warring planets. Alana is from the planet Landfall, a place of great technological advancement, and their planet is in constant conflict with the magic-wielders of that planet's moon Wreath, where Marko hails from. Marko was captured after a battle with Landfall where he threw down his arms because he is a self-proclaimed conscientious objector, and he was imprisoned, where Alana, who was also somewhat disgraced in her military service, is his prison guard. Fast-forward several months, and Alana is giving birth to their child, Hazel, the actual narrator of the series. The very beginning of the series is dedicated to her actually giving birth, and it's hilarious and beautiful and suspenseful since they are being pursued from both sides of this galaxy-wide conflict. In the midst of their pursuit, where they are in search of a legendary (and perhaps fictional) Rocketship Forest so that they might get off the planet with their new baby, we are introduced to all sorts of new, strange, frightening and wonderfully rich characters. We meet Prince Robot IV, a humanoid being with what appears to be an old-fashioned TV set for a head, who is the one primarily tasked with the elimination of Marko and Alana. We also meet two of the bounty hunters that have are in a bidding war to get the fugitives, The Will and The Stalk. The Stalk is a somewhat frightening-looking spider-like alien who carries weapons in all of her additional appendages, and The Will is humanoid, but has with him what might be one of my favorite characters EVER and that is the character of Lying Cat, who is a talking cat that can tell when someone is lying. Also characters in their own right are a legendary group of terrifying killers called The Horrors, which, like all characters in this book aren't quite what they seem.<br /><br />Alana is much more prone to using violent means to protect her husband and new baby, but Marko is hesitant, despite that he carries a sword chained inside its sheath that he has vowed never to unsheath. Prince Robot IV is less than pleased with the notion of having to track this fugitive couple down, feeling that it is beneath his aristocratic sensibilities. The Stalk and The Will obviously have a romantic/sexual past but both of them are fearsome, amoral killers, or as one might think, until The Will goes to the pleasure planet of Sextillion and his unethical code is put to the test.<br /><br />Vaughan's writing is as strong and natural as it's ever been, and he uses a great deal of English-Speaking slang, because... why not? Whatever language these different races might be speaking to each other is still going to be translated to our language, and Vaughan, if nothing else, is masterful at the art of language. Each issue moves along at an incredibly even pace, allowing for moments of great suspense and violence, but also allowing for great humor, character beats and texture to the worlds around them. If there is a science fiction-fantasy comic out there that is so adept at creating its own universe, I have yet to read it.<br /><br />Fiona Staples' art is an excellent balance of extreme detail to abstract lines and shading. It is exactly the feel that the book demands, and once again, Vaughan has found the perfect person to illustrate his work. Her amazeballs work is absolutely suited to this book and is absolutely one of those artists that demands following in this and other titles.<br /><br />The thing that this book may bring up for some people is an age-old argument about Art vs. Pornography. The book as a whole is certainly not for the kiddies, but it's hardly packed with sexual activity. However, there is a book in this collection that broaches that argument because it does take place on the planet of Sextillion and as The Will goes walking down its streets, there are several rather explicit sexual acts going on in the background. The general way of defining \\"pornography\\" is showing sexual acts that are designed to titillate or create a general state of sexual arousal. That is very much not the case here. This is not a comic that is somehow designed to sexually excite its readers, but I can imagine perhaps a less open-minded reader or someone who is new to reading mature-themed comics being a more than a little taken aback by some of those panels. But really, it's parsed down to no more than three or four panels. It's not gratuitous and it's not in your face, but it's there, so new readers may want to be aware. That's all I really wanted to say about that.<br /><br />When it all boils down to how this book is and how it makes you feel, it's an beautiful and massively entertaining book that keeps you invested in all of its characters, creates a incredibly rich universe for these characters to exist in, never fails to please the eye or the intellect, and really, like Hazel, SAGA is something of a small miracle.-fantasy comic out there that is so adept at creating its own universe, I have yet to read it.<br /><br />Fiona Staples' art is an excellent balance of extreme detail to abstract lines and shading. It is exactly the feel that the book demands, and once again, Vaughan has found the perfect person to illustrate his work. Her amazeballs work is absolutely suited to this book and is absolutely one of those artists that demands following in this and other titles.<br /><br />The thing that this book may bring up for some people is an age-old argument about Art vs. Pornography. B003TSEEDY|4.0|This is another book that I picked up right before the movie came out and only picked it up because the movie looked like one I'd want to eventually see. It's the second time this year that I've done that (Extremely Loud and Incredibly Close was the first time), and it's the second time I've been well pleased and satisfied by a great book.<br /><br />I'd heard of the book before. Shortly after it came out, a coworker was reading it on her lunch break many years ago and laughing out loud.  I'm not sure why I never read it until now, but I'm certainly glad I did. Another friend says the book chooses you when it wants to be read, and if that's true then this book came at an opportune moment when I needed it most.<br /><br />You could say this book helps to define its generation. Stephen Chbosky, the author, tells the story of an introverted boy named Charlie about to enter high school.  He is quickly taken under the wing of two sibling seniors, Sam and Patrick, and accepted into their group of friends. Making mix-tapes for each other, reading good books, going to parties, first kisses, drinking and smoking, writing essays in English class, going to football games, riding around; the list goes on and on of the things that define a teenager's life and become important to them while an \\"adult\\" future seems so far away. Numerous songs and books are mentioned throughout the book (I was pleased to find a mix tape-like CD for the soundtrack to the movie) and whether you've heard them before or read them, you instantly connect to Charlie and start reminiscing of your own glory days.  I know I did.<br /><br />It's the way the story is shaped and told that makes it even more special.  The entire book is a series of letters that Charlie writes to an unnamed friend. Each is dated from 1990 through 1992 and starts out \\"Dear Friend,\\" and ends with \\"Love always, Charlie.\\"  So, you almost feel like you've found Charlie's letters in a trunk and you are spying on his past, or maybe you are the friend he wrote them to.  Either way, they are poetic and haunting at times, but always innocent and frequently humorous. The tone is very passive and friendly but through his words, we see Charlie worrying about his friend's love interests, discovering Patrick is gay, experimenting with drugs, taking up smoking, going to see and participating in the Rocky Horror Picture Show, breaking a girl's heart, getting his driver's license, and much more.<br /><br />As someone who prefers a deep absorbing book I easily suspended all expectations and accepted the lack of details. Charlie is a true teenage voice, filled with all the angst a kid at that age experiences.  I entered high school around that same time and experienced the same roller coaster of emotions Charlie found himself going through.  Charlie was someone I knew, and at times while reading, I was Charlie.<br /><br />While the book focuses mainly on Charlie's friendships with Sam and Patrick, we are introduced to an array of other characters that easily represent the typical stereotypes that define your peer group - the jock, the girl who talks too much, the hip guy that graduated last year who still throws parties for everyone, the cheating boyfriend, and so on. I also found myself underlining numerous heart-felt quotes in the text that I'm sure were and still are destined to be found in someone's high school graduation speech.<br /><br />While it is a book that has been frequently challenged for its mention of drug use, bullying, sexual identity, and so on, I think this is a book that is very true to real life. I know if I was a teacher, I'd want to see my students reading it.<br /><br />THE MOVIE:<br /><br />Concerning the film, I'm looking forward to seeing Emma Watson shine as Sam. Most will know her as Hermione from the Harry Potter series, but I have a feeling she will become known for much more as she matures as an actress. I could have easily seen Drew Berrymore in this role years ago. Ezra Miller (We Need To Talk About Kevin and Californication), while not known extremely well will surely do well as Patrick. And let's not forget the adorable Logan Lerman as Charlie. You'll recognize him as Percy Jackson from the 2010 film.  The three together look marvelous on the movie poster as a possibly new 80s-esque bratpack? Only time and film will tell.r (We Need To Talk About Kevin and Californication), while not known extremely well will surely do well as Patrick. 0812550706|5.0|I am not going to write a very lengthy review, but wanted to add another positive note.  I was recommended this novel based on my love of a few things:  the novels of Ray Bradbury, dystopian novels and films, Star Trek, and Battle Royale (the Japanese novel).  I hesitated for a few years because of the Last Starfighter looking cover and the 6-12 year old protagonist.  I admit I was judging the book by its cover.  But finally too many folks told me to read it, so I did, and I am very happy that I did.<br /><br />In this innovate work of science fiction, Ender, a young boy called a '3rd' (because he is the 3rd child born to the couple despite the limit of 2 children) is taken in by the military to be trained as a soldier from a young age.  Early on the reader discovers that Ender is special, and that a nameless narrator of sorts speaks of grand plans for the boy.  We follow Ender through his years of training and the 'games' he must continually take part in as part of his training.<br /><br />There are a few things that make this novel a breakthrough and relevant even today.  The fact that this 1977 novel was published the same year as Star Wars is pretty amazing.  In the novel folks use the 'nets' quite a bit.  The author even makes references to people creating online accounts and changing their personality profiles to hide their identities and such.  Pretty amazing for a 70s novel!  Like Bradbury with his earlier version of Facebook (seriously, read Fahrenheit 451 and pay attention to Guy's wife!), Orson Scott Card had a somewhat reasonable view of the future, albeit with space travel and aliens.  But the virtual reality games and other little additions are intriguing and enjoyable to read about.<br /><br />Despite some obvious 'childlike' moments such as people calling each other 'fart eaters', the book has a more adult style that I found appealing.  I could really imagine the book originally being targeted to the teenage to 20 something crowd.  But the depth of the writing, the engaging characters, and the complex themes are appealing even to someone older.  It's not on the same level as, say, 1984 or Brave New World, but this is one of the standouts of science fiction.  I do prefer the style of Philip K Dick to that of Orson Scott Card, but once I'm done with Dick's works, I plan on spending a little more time with Card's other works.he writing, the engaging characters, and the complex themes are appealing even to someone older. 0451232852|5.0|FALL OF GIANTS is a masterful historical novel of the early years of the Twentieth century from the build up to World War One through the Russian Revolution to the women's suffrage movement in England. The book follows several families across several continents, the Dewars in the U.S., the Fitzherberts in England, the Von Ulrichs in Germany, the Peshkovs in Russia, and the Williamses in Wales. Follett does a terrific job of keeping the plot moving and intertwining the lives of the main characters. It is intended to be the first of a trilogy covering the century.<br /><br />The touches of history were fascinating, and I especially enjoyed the scenes showing the madness of warfare during the Great War. Near the conclusion of the novel, the mention of Adolph Hitler, foreshadowing the next book, was well done. The theme, encapsulated in the title, of the fall of the Tsars and of the ruling elite in England and the rise of the working classes was engaging.<br /><br />Mr. Follett is a top-notch thriller writer, but I feel these historicals are his strong point. To me THE PILLARS OF THE EARTH is one of the best novels I have ever read. It was so good that I was hesitant to read its sequel, WORLD WITHOUT END, because I feared he could never come near it. In truth, I was disappointed in the sequel, but only in comparing it to PILLARS. How could I expect him to repeat a masterpiece? For the same reason I was hesitant to tackle FALL OF GIANTS.<br /><br />But Mr. Follett has produced another winner; well-researched and well-written. The characters are well drawn and engaging.<br /><br />But let me be clear. This novel is not a thriller nor is it meant to be. But as a historical saga, heavy on the history, it is excellent.

1439152802|5.0|Kate Morton's \\"The Secret Keeper\\" takes place in London in the 1940's, rural England during the early sixties, and Suffolk in 2011. At the heart of this complex work is a shocking event witnessed by sixteen-year old Laurel Nicolson. Should she reveal what she saw or remain silent? If she decides to tell the authorities the truth, the consequences could be devastating.<br /><br />Fifty years pass. Laurel, who has had a successful acting career, takes a break from her busy schedule to join her siblings at the bedside of their ailing mother. It is a time not only of joy (the three sisters reminisce about their happy childhood), but also of unspeakable sadness. Dorothy Nicolson is dying, and as she lies in her hospital bed, bits and pieces from her early life come back to haunt her. Laurel, who senses that her mother has hidden important facts about her past from her family, embarks on a mission to learn the truth. What she discovers will shed new light on the personality of a good-hearted wife and mother who cared for her husband and children with tenderness and devotion.<br /><br />The flashbacks to forties London are spellbinding. Dorothy Smithson, nicknamed Dolly, is an orphan who is obsessed with attaining a lovely home, nice clothing, and social status. She has a handsome but impoverished boyfriend named Jimmy. In addition, Dolly meets Vivien Jenkins, a wealthy married woman who appears to have it all. Dorothy's envy, Jimmy's adoration and loyalty, Vivien's hidden demons, and German bombers during the Blitz will help determine the fate of these three people.<br /><br />The author's gorgeous descriptive writing and meticulous character development draw us into a world far different from our own. In 1940s London, citizens used ration cards to purchase groceries, listened for air raid sirens to warn of impending attacks, and wondered if they would survive the war. They longed for a future free of bomb blasts, anti-aircraft fire, and perpetual fear. Meanwhile, Dolly, Jimmy, Vivien, and her husband, Henry, are on a collision course, their fates intertwined.<br /><br />At times, the novel is sluggishly paced and the conclusion is weakened by contrived elements that strain credulity. Still, readers will find much to like in this heartfelt exploration of love, loss, and redemption, set against the backdrop of a country in turmoil. Morton's startling revelations in the book's final pages force us to reconsider everything we thought we knew about Dorothy and Vivien. \\"The Secret Keeper\\" casts an undeniable spell and Morton's central theme is one that we would all be wise to embrace: \\"Don't wait too long to realize what's important.\\" (Four and a half stars rounded up to five.)Jimmy, Vivien, and her husband, Henry, are on a collision course, their fates intertwined.<br /><br />At times, the novel is sluggishly paced and the conclusion is weakened by contrived elements that strain credulity. The thing is: who cares?  Without giving anything away, should you still attempt to get through this tragedy of literature, I didn't care that the council seat of this 50-person town was empty.  I didn't care that some people wanted to include the \\"Fields\\" (essentially the ghetto of the town) into another town and some didn't.  Rowling just didn't make me understand why it mattered.<br /><br />Further, I was finally forced to admit something to myself that I have been avoiding for years:  at a literary level, Rowling is just not a good writer.  Her sentences are short, meaningless, and lack depth and perceptive.  I happily ignored this in HP because they are, after all, young adult books, but now that she is writing for us grown ups, her writing style needs to reflect that, and sadly, itdidn't.<br /><br />A Casual Vacancy just did not live up to my expectations, which admittedly, may have been too high.  I am truly disappointed.

0385536828|5.0|Ian McEwan has done it again.  He has written a brilliant novel from a woman's point of view, similar to the style he used in Atonement.  The story is about Serena Frome (rhymes with Plume), a young woman who finds herself recruited for a post in the M-16, England's variant of the CIA.  She gets a lowly post there but finds herself involved in an interesting project called `Sweet Tooth'.<br /><br />Sweet Tooth's primary function is to channel money through various non-profits that cannot be traced and provide stipends to artists who they hope will produce work sympathetic to the cause.  One of the artists is a writer named Tom Healy and Serena is chosen to be the one to court him.  The writers get 20,000 pounds per year as a stipend, which is quite a lot for the 1970's which is when this novel takes place.  Serena is beautiful, Cambridge educated and she and Tom Healy quickly fall into an exciting love affair.<br /><br />In the 1970's, and even now for all I know, those recruited for the M-16 needed to come from the right schools, have the correct background, and be from upper class families.  Most were male, and females were the exception, left to lowly jobs like clerks or secretaries.  There were the one or two affirmative action employees but they were the minority.<br /><br />Before coming to M-16, Serena was involved with an older man named Tony Canning who groomed her for the position and put in a word for her.  He had connections with the agency which opened the door for Serena.<br /><br />The novel is filled with twists and turns.  It is not exactly a spy story, not exactly a love story, and not exactly a political novel.  It combines the elements of all three in a wonderful way.  The book is a literary page-turner in the best sense.  I had trouble putting it down once I picked it up and it gave me a lot more than I ever expected.<br /><br />I recommend this for fans of McEwan, of which I am one, for those who have never read McEwan before and want to start - this is an excellent first peek into his writing, and for anyone who likes literary page-turners that are filled with fine writing  and make it difficult for you to come up for air.- this is an excellent first peek into his writing, and for anyone who likes literary page-turners that are filled with fine writing  and make it difficult for you to come up for air.

160774273X|5.0|Even if you own dozens of bread books, as I do, you will find much to love about Ken Forkish's \\"Flour, Water, Salt, Yeast.\\" One of my biggest difficulties in making bread at home has always been scoring the top of the loaf before baking it. Even when I use an oiled razor blade, I can't seem to make a clean cut in the top of the bread. The blade tugs at the dough and causes it to deflate. Ken Forkish solves this problem by recommending that you bake your bread with the seam side (as opposed to the smooth side) up and not scoring it at all. The natural fissures in the seam side of the bread will open up in the heat of the oven and create the same kind of effect you'd get from successfully scoring the top of the loaf. This information alone made the book worthwhile to me. The critics who complain that the book contains only a handful of recipes are correct but they are missing the point. This isn't one of those \\"1000 Ways To Make Bread at Home\\" books. This book is about the fundamentals of good home breadmaking.It is well-written, well-illustrated, and bursting with good advice. I recommend it heartily.

0525952926|4.0|Winter Of The World is the second mammoth-size work of historical fiction in  Ken Follett's The Century Trilogy that kept me very engrossed. Winter Of The World follows the lives of five main characters, each the child of the five families featured in Fall Of Giants, as they move through events beginning with the rise of the Third Reich, through the Spanish Civil War and the great dramas of WWII, up to the explosions of the American and Soviet atomic bombs.<br /><br />Follett's characters are developed well enough and his narrative abilities are  strong enough to enable me to feel that that I was right along side each of the main characters as they move through the major events in their lives. I recommend Winter Of The World to those who enjoy historical fiction and think they would enjoy traveling with Follett's characters as they move through the years that were filled with social, political and emotional turmoil.<br /><br />Is Winter Of The World a perfect book? Of course not; it has its share of limitations. For example, Follett's dialogue, at times, does not ring true and the historical situations in which he involves his characters often appear to be too coincidental and expected. These limitations, however, are mostly outweighed by the excellent job Follett does in pacing his book and in creating a \\"you are there\\" atmosphere for the reader.<br /><br />I'm pretty sure you'll find Winter Of The World to be a very entertaining, well-researched and memorable book. But be aware that your enjoyment won't come cheap -- the retail price of Winter Of The World is $36 in hardcover and $19.99 electronically. I think, however, you'll find that it is worth the money and your time investment.

0345803493|1.0|In the second installment of this unholy trinity, E.L. James somehow finds a way to take what was tolerable in the first novel and completely replace it with a giant steaming pile of crap.<br /><br />The entire \\"plot\\" consists of the same interaction between Ana and Christian except that the author mixes it up by putting the characters in different physical locations. The routine is as follows:<br /><br />Ana: OMG Christian is great but I am just a mousy blob, will I ever be enough for him?<br />Christian: Seethe. My past is dark but I will explain my issues to you because they are straight out of a freshman psychology course.<br />Ana: OMG those are all terrible qualities that you possess but I will overlook them because you had a bad childhood and I am going to rescue you with my love.<br /><br />Then there is a perfunctory exchange where Ana tries to pretend that she has free will because it gets Christian hard. And then they run off somewhere and bone.<br /><br />This same pattern occurs in any number of interesting places: a boat, Ana's apartment, Christian's apartment, the bathtub, probably another boat, a fancy-pants masquerade ball. Our author seems to equate a change of scenery with a change of subject matter, and that if she makes these people go to enough interesting places then the people reading this tripe will forget that neither Ana nor Christian has developed one iota as a character or a human being. By this point, all the secondary characters have all but disappeared except when they show up to either a) cause Ana to feel possessive/triumphant/vindictive because every straight lady in the universe either wants to do it with Christian or has done it with Christian (there are no other options); b) incite Christian's irrational jealousy because every straight man in the universe wants to do it with this insipid blob of a woman he calls Ana (there are no other options); or c) to cause the happy couple inconvenience and/or extreme pain (because when you're rich and beautiful everyone is out to get you). It seems like other people are only there to prevent these two from doing it, even those who are family and friends. BECAUSE ALL THAT MATTERS IS THAT THEY ARE DOING IT ALL THE TIME.<br /><br />And speaking of doing it, even though the boning scenes are frequent and often lengthy, reading them became less of a thrill and more of a chore. Maybe it would be more entertaining if the prose didn't read like a 14 year old's journal. Ana has this weird pattern of blending sterile, almost scientific terms with giggly little euphemisms. No. One. Talks. Like. That. I guarantee that you could find more risque material on the iPhone of any given sexually active 20-something. The worst, and I mean the absolute worst, is Ana's internal monologue. Or dialogue, if you buy into that inner goddess crap. She sounds like she is six years old and has just discovered shiny things. Every time she said \\"Holy crap\\" or \\"Jeez\\" I wanted to slap her across the face. Christian is only slightly better, though I suspect that's because his internal thoughts remain a mystery. If the author has such a hard time writing for someone with the same type of reproductive organs that she has, then I shudder to think what she thinks a man sounds like.<br /><br />Another point at issue here is that this book is simply unbelievable, even for a work of fiction. Normally every good story has a central plot and conflicts that are tertiary and reasonably related to the central struggle at issue. However, this book seems to completely ignore that trend and instead insert a thousand different phenomenal side plots. It would have been fine if the story had focused on the relationship between Christian and his now-stalker ex-sub and saved the stupid helicopter crash business for the next novel. NO. She had to smash it all into one volume so that she would have room to write even more ridiculous crap in the next one.<br /><br />Between the parties, the boning, the running away from crazy stalkers, and Ana's insufferable internal dialogue, I am surprised that either of them have any time to be contributing members of society. But wait a minute! They are not. Christian runs a vague business that makes tons of money but for some reason doesn't require him to show up to the office ever. Ana is supposedly some kind of deep, literary wunderkind but she spends all day getting bent out of shape over Christian and being sexually harassed by her boss (SPOILER: every man in this book who isn't Christian or any member of his family is up to no good). The world is effin awesome because Christian can buy anything he wants and can shower Ana with ridiculous gifts that she claims she doesn't want but never refuses. Also, the brand-name dropping in this book bugs me for some reason. I understand that certain products denote luxury and thus the presence of said products is consistent with the theme of the generous lover. However, it seems that the author is name-dropping so much because she couldn't be bothered to actually put any thought into the description of the items. We all know what Macs, iPads, and Louboutins look like so the author doesn't have to go through the inconvenience of actually describing the thing. It's lazy but it's consistent with the theme of telling rather than showing. The same is true of Ana's description of the various humanoids in her life, especially Christian. She is the one who tells us if they are attractive. When someone asks me to intimately describe the man I love I say that his nose is rounded and a little downward sloping like his father's and that he has worry lines on his face from years of combat but when he smiles they disappear. Apparently all Ana can come up with is \\"Holy Moses, Christian is so hot!!!\\"  Her descriptions are basic and sterile and immature and make for a terrible, terrible read. When I read all her hackneyed attempts at poetic descriptions I almost felt embarrassed for her. If this woman isan English major then I weep for the arts.<br /><br />But E, you say, why does it bother you that this book is a pantload? Can't you let it be crap-in-a-vacuum and do something more constructive with your time than write this review? The answer is, it bothers me because this is not crap-in-a-vacuum. This is a global phenomenon and it is bound to make an impact one way or another. I am also not opposed to people creating works that have have no real philosophical value. The danger occurs when hordes of impressionable adolescents are exposed to this garbage and walk away thinking that the relationship between these two cardboard cutouts is something to be admired and sought after. It's not that we should be afraid that teenagers or anyone with an impressionable mind will get caught up in kinky sex and end up as someone's love slave. The dysfunction is in the psychological relationship.<br /><br />Christian is controlling and manipulative but those qualities are touted as virtues and we are supposed to believe that this is simply heightened concern and ultimately love for Ana. This is not love. A little bit of jealousy is any relationship is pretty common, and there are situations where it may be right to take a stand when a partner is behaving recklessly. However, this does not extend to things like: preventing your partner from working if that's what they want to do, dictating how they dress, telling them who they should and should not associate with. I have known people in abusive relationships and all this behavior sounds eerily familiar. Christian is controlling every aspect of Ana's life without giving her any real say in his. She can't hang out with any male friends, but he is allowed to have his ex-dom as a business partner. Any protests are dismissed and I can't help but feel bad for Ana. I feel less bad when I hear all the terrible excuses she makes for his behavior. He was abused as a child. His formative sexual experiences were sort of traumatizing. WE GETIT. This is no excuse for him to grow up to be a controlling and manipulative jerk. He may be a terrible person but you are the one who is enabling this unacceptable behavior. Now we have millions of girls and women reading this who are going to think this kind of crap is okay because some handsome billionaire in a cheap novel does it. This just goes to show that anything can be done in the name of love and people will swallow it whole. This not only undercuts women who have fought long and hard to make it in careers and to take care of their families without constantly being rescued, but it also does a giant disservice to the Joses of the world: nice guys who are a little misguided and are kicked to the curb because of handsome monsters like Christian.<br /><br />I can't believe I wasted my time reading this abortion. Not only is the writing terrible, the sex repetitive and boring, and the plot completely unbelievable: this book also sends a really dangerous message to anyone who reads it, namely it is okay if he abuses you, as long as he says he loves you.

I really can't wait for ebooks to fully take over the world and prices to align with delivery costs...<br /><br />But that gripe aside, 'The Hydrogen Sonata' is another Culture novel and for me, as with '[[ASIN:0316123412 Surface Detail (Culture)]]', it seems that Banks is running out of meaningful things to say in this milieu.<br /><br />Of course, Banks can write and his output is prodigious!<br /><br />So I've no qualms with how he tells a story and he is deft with emotional content and descriptive prose. But I think back to earlier, tighter Culture novels such as '[[ASIN:0553575376 Excession]]' and 'The Hydrogen Sonata' seems overwrought in the words per plot arc metric.<br /><br />In particular, the underlying question of whether religious truth would cast enough doubt for a whole society of billions upon billions to change their collective minds about Subliming seemed a shaky edifice to support all the activity as various characters - Minds, Human and Alien - jostle for their little slice of control.<br /><br />Plus, the motivations for the somewhat bad guy were a hazy for me. Indeed, quite a few of the motivations were hazy, but instead of setting up tension in the novel, it was just annoying because I could not get with the program and cheer the characters on.<br /><br />And some of the calculations seemed a bit off. A 200km long ship housing more people than a planet? Or the continual references to how unknowable the Sublime is and how if you Sublime even hours late you'll be out of sync with the rest of your culture because things change so radically. Given that a single universal clock is not possible, I did not understand how a whole species wouldn't be out of sync on that basis. And one Mind comes back (kind of) to live an enigmatic life within the substrate of another Mind. Why exactly? I really missed the point of that little subtext. Then there was a terrible continuity gap about 50% in which was the result of missing text. And if you've not, there are no 'spoiler alerts' of earlier novels such that you have to read them in sequence, so it's actually a good place to start. 0071752854|4.0|Pitch Anything is great.<br /><br />It's one of the few books that are true to its title -- you can actually use it to pitch anything.<br /><br />While Oren's background is in the high-stake world of capital investments, I'm actually using the book to help land and negotiate my dream job, and am getting incredible results.<br /><br />I've done sales in the past and the framework presented here is quite different than what you would expect. It's counterintuitive, but shockingly effective. It's a fresh approach, and the frames, or the roles we all play at one time or another, Oren describes are true to life. Once you recognize them, you'll start picking up on them everywhere.<br /><br />If you've studied social dynamics in the past or Clotaire Rapaille's reptilian marketing, you'll be familiar with where some of the concepts in Pitch Anything come from. What this book does is that it has applied them to the business world.<br /><br />The stories in the book are really great. They resonate deeply with situations I've been in and I'm sure you'll identify with them as well. If you've ever wondered why office lobbies are designed the way they are, you'll find out in this book.<br /><br />The only downside to the material is that it can be difficult to assume some of the frames just from reading. It will take some practice and it's helpful to see if you can find personal or fictional examples to see how they operate in the wild to model the tonality and body language.<br /><br />All said, I can see this becoming one of the classic sales books and its no surprise that it has been added to the Personal MBA reading list.

Chi Mai Vivra Per Sempre is a crossover version of Queen's \\"Who Wants to Live Forever\\". Finally, La Prima Volta is a crossover version of \\"The First Time Ever I Saw Your Face\\", probably most famously covered by Roberta Flack. J&C do every bit of justice to the song, making it just as delicate and loving in Italian.<br /><br />Listen to the last notes in The Prayer and Forse. That is an industrial strength voice. And he's 17.<br /><br />This is one of the few reviews I've ever put on Amazon, but I think this highly of the album and of the two people who made it. B00005MK9Y|5.0|Bob Marley & The Wailers reggae sound and Rastafarian message had carried over extremely well on his first few solo albums.  Than they deliver their first album,simply called [[ASIN:B00005KB9W Live!]]. The response to that was so strong,it was clear they'd be delivering another. Though his music and message of Bob Marley had already gotten the attention of millions of people already his audience increased ten fold with reggae's wholesale embrace from the UK punk rock scene. That and the overwhelming crossover success of the [[ASIN:B000PC1JKS Exodus]] album meant that,through record sales,the message of global unity Marley embraced would reach the people even more than it already had. So the second live album he did with the Wailers very much reflected this.<br /><br />  Rather than presented his songs as separate entities as the Wailers' studio albums often did,this album presents Bob Marley's music as separate parts of a much larger whole. That often seems to be the intent of reggae music and the humanistic message it carries. Yet the live setting,with Bob and the audience occasionally exchange call and response messages between each other,really brings  elongated versions of classics like \\"Exodus\\",\\"Concrete Jungle\\" and \\"Rat Race\\",a song composed by Bob's wife Rita to life. Of course one of the main highlights here is the medley of \\"War/No More Trouble\\",where the earnestness of the songs and the value of peace to humanity is firmly articulated to a willing and wanting audience.<br /><br />  On \\"Punky Reggae Party\\"? Well there Bob may in fact be illustrating the most important aspect about this album. Punk followers of the day had as much to say about racial,economic and arms conflicts as Bob Marley did. But most punks were very angry and often hopeless that the conditions they disliked could ever change. So they became loud,aggressive rebels. Alas Bob Marley and the Wailers were,in their own culture just as socially rebellious. On the other hand in Rastafarianism they had a spiritual back round illuminating their way,one that gave them a more positive outlook that the conditions they disliked could be changed for the better. The connection between the band,and the audience who were more than likely consisting mostly of punk fans,created a musical dichotomy that not only resonated musically but perhaps gave some of these angry young people more of a mission regarding their cause.hey had a spiritual back round illuminating their way,one that gave them a more positive outlook that the conditions they disliked could be changed for the better.
